<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41586"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The Apostasia genome and the evolution of orchids"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2017 549:7672"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2017-09-13"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2017 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="WebComparing the whole genome sequence of Apostasia shenzhenica with transcriptome and genome data from five orchid subfamilies permits the reconstruction of an ancestral gene toolkit, providing insight into orchid origins, evolution and diversification. Around 10 per cent of flowering plant species are orchids, with a broad diversity in both morphology and lifestyle. Apostasia is one of the earliest-diverging genera of Orchidaceae. To study the evolution and diversity of Orchidaceae, Zhong-Jian Liu, Yves Van de Peer and colleagues sequenced the genome of Apostasia shenzhenica, a self-pollinating species found in southeast China. The authors also report improved genomes for two species of Epidendroideae, Phalaenopsis equestris and Dendrobium catenatum, as well as transcriptome analysis of representatives of subfamilies of Orchidaceae. Their analyses provide insights into orchid origins, genome evolution, adaptation and diversification. Constituting approximately 10% of flowering plant species, orchids (Orchidaceae) display unique flower morphologies, possess an extraordinary diversity in lifestyle, and have successfully colonized almost every habitat on Earth1,2,3. Here we report the draft genome sequence of Apostasia shenzhenica4, a representative of one of two genera that form a sister lineage to the rest of the Orchidaceae, providing a reference for inferring the genome content and structure of the most recent common ancestor of all extant orchids and improving our understanding of their origins and evolution. In addition, we present transcriptome data for representatives of Vanilloideae, Cypripedioideae and Orchidoideae, and novel third-generation genome data for two species of Epidendroideae, covering all five orchid subfamilies. A. shenzhenica shows clear evidence of a whole-genome duplication, which is shared by all orchids and occurred shortly before their divergence. Comparisons between A. shenzhenica and other orchids and angiosperms also permitted the reconstruction of an ancestral orchid gene toolkit. We identify new gene families, gene family expansions and contractions, and changes within MADS-box gene classes, which control a diverse suite of developmental processes, during orchid evolution. This study sheds new light on the genetic mechanisms underpinning key orchid innovations, including the development of the labellum and gynostemium, pollinia, and seeds without endosperm, as well as the evolution of epiphytism; reveals relationships between the Orchidaceae subfamilies; and helps clarify the evolutionary history of orchids within the angiosperms."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs3" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs3">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs3-content"><p>Constituting approximately 10% of flowering plant species, orchids (Orchidaceae) display unique flower morphologies, possess an extraordinary diversity in lifestyle, and have successfully colonized almost every habitat on Earth<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Roberts, D. L. &amp; Dixon, K. W. Orchids. Curr. Biol. 18, R325âR329 (2008)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1032">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Givnish, T. J. et al. Orchid phylogenomics and multiple drivers of their extraordinary diversification. Proc. R. Soc. B 282, 1553 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1035">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Givnish, T. J. et al. Orchid historical biogeography, diversification, Antarctica and the paradox of orchid dispersal. J. Biogeogr. 43, 1905â1916 (2016)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1038">3</a></sup>. Here we report the draft genome sequence of <i>Apostasia shenzhenica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Chen, L. J. &amp; Liu, Z. J. Apostasia shenzhenica: a new species of Apostasioideae (Orchidaceae) from China. Plant Science Journal 29, 38â41 (2011)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1044">4</a></sup>, a representative of one of two genera that form a sister lineage to the rest of the Orchidaceae, providing a reference for inferring the genome content and structure of the most recent common ancestor of all extant orchids and improving our understanding of their origins and evolution. In addition, we present transcriptome data for representatives of Vanilloideae, Cypripedioideae and Orchidoideae, and novel third-generation genome data for two species of Epidendroideae, covering all five orchid subfamilies. <i>A. shenzhenica</i> shows clear evidence of a whole-genome duplication, which is shared by all orchids and occurred shortly before their divergence. Comparisons between <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and other orchids and angiosperms also permitted the reconstruction of an ancestral orchid gene toolkit. We identify new gene families, gene family expansions and contractions, and changes within MADS-box gene classes, which control a diverse suite of developmental processes, during orchid evolution. This study sheds new light on the genetic mechanisms underpinning key orchid innovations, including the development of the labellum and gynostemium, pollinia, and seeds without endosperm, as well as the evolution of epiphytism; reveals relationships between the Orchidaceae subfamilies; and helps clarify the evolutionary history of orchids within the angiosperms.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The Apostasioideae are a small subfamily of orchids that includes only two genera (<i>Apostasia</i> and <i>Neuwiedia</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Givnish, T. J. et al. Orchid phylogenomics and multiple drivers of their extraordinary diversification. Proc. R. Soc. B 282, 1553 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1104">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Kocyan, A., Qiu, Y.-L., Endress, P. K. &amp; Conti, E. A phylogenetic analysis of Apostasioideae (Orchidaceae) based on ITS, trnL-F and matK sequences. Plant Syst. Evol. 247, 203â213 (2004)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1107">5</a></sup>), consisting of terrestrial species confined to the humid areas of southeast Asia, Japan, and northern Australia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Dressler, R. L. Phylogeny and Classification of the Orchid Family (Discorides, 1993)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1111">6</a></sup>. Although Apostasioideae share some synapomorphies with other orchids (for example, small seeds with a reduced embryo and a myco-heterotrophic protocorm stage), they possess several unique traits, the most conspicuous of which is their floral morphology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kocyan, A. &amp; Endress, P. K. Floral structure and development of Apostasia and Neuwiedia (Apostasioideae) and their relationships to other Orchidaceae. Int. J. Plant Sci. 162, 847â867 (2001)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1115">7</a></sup>. <i>Apostasia</i> has a non-resupinate, solanum-type flower with anthers closely encircling the stigma (including postgenital fusion), a long ovary, and an actinomorphic perianth with an undifferentiated labellum. Three stamens (two of which are fertile) are basally fused to the style, forming a relatively simple gynostemium, and the anthers contain powdery pollen (grains not unified into pollinia). These characteristics (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig5">Extended Data Fig. 1a</a>) differ from those of other Orchidaceae subfamilies, which have three sepals, three petals (of which one has specialized to form the labellum), and stamens and pistil fused into a more complex gynostemium (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig5">Extended Data Fig. 1b</a>), but are similar to those of some species of Hypoxidaceae (a sister family to Orchidaceae, in the order Asparagales).</p><p>We sequenced the <i>A. shenzhenica</i> genome using a combination of different approaches; the total length of the final assembly was 349âMb (see Methods and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Tables 1â4</a>). We confidently annotated 21,841 protein-coding genes, of which 20,202 (92.50%) were supported by transcriptome data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 5</a>). Using single-copy orthologues, we performed a BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1144">8</a></sup> assessment that indicated that the completeness of the genome was 93.62%, suggesting that the <i>A. shenzhenica</i> genome assembly is of high quality (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 6</a>). For comparative analyses, we also improved the quality of the previously published genome assemblies of the orchids <i>Phalaenopsis equestris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Cai, J. et al. The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. Nat. Genet. 47, 65â72 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1157">9</a></sup> and <i>Dendrobium catenatum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zhang, G.-Q. et al. The Dendrobium catenatum Lindl. genome sequence provides insights into polysaccharide synthase, floral development and adaptive evolution. Sci. Rep. 6, 19029 (2016)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1163">10</a></sup> (see Methods and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Tables 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">7</a>).</p><p>We constructed a high-confidence phylogenetic tree and estimated the divergence times of 15 plant species using genes extracted from a total of 439 single-copy families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig6">Extended Data Fig. 2</a>). We undertook a computational analysis of gene family sizes (CAFÃ 2.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="De Bie, T ., Cristianini, N ., Demuth, J. P &amp; Hahn, M. W. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics 22, 1269â1271 (2006)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1183">11</a></sup>) to study gene family expansion and contraction during the evolution of orchids and related species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 1.1</a>). By comparing 12 plant species, we found 474 gene families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig7">Extended Data Fig. 3</a>) that appeared unique to orchids (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 1.2</a>). Gene Ontology and Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) enrichment analysis found these gene families to be specifically enriched in the terms â<i>O</i>-methyltransferase activityâ, âcysteine-type peptidase activityâ, âflavone and flavonol biosynthesisâ and âstilbenoid, diarylheptanoid and gingerol biosynthesisâ (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 1.2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Phylogenetic tree showing divergence times and the evolution of gene family sizes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Phylogenetic tree showing divergence times and the evolution of gene family sizes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature23897/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature23897/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature23897_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature23897/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature23897_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="789" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>The phylogenetic tree shows the topology and divergence times for 15 plant species. As expected, as a member of the Apostasioideae, <i>A. shenzhenica</i> is sister to all other orchids. In general, the estimated orchid divergence times are in good agreement with recent broad scale orchid phylogenies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Givnish, T. J. et al. Orchid phylogenomics and multiple drivers of their extraordinary diversification. Proc. R. Soc. B 282, 1553 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1221">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Givnish, T. J. et al. Orchid historical biogeography, diversification, Antarctica and the paradox of orchid dispersal. J. Biogeogr. 43, 1905â1916 (2016)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1224">3</a></sup>. Divergence times are indicated by light blue bars at the internodes; the range of these bars indicates the 95% confidence interval of the divergence time. Numbers at branches indicate the expansion and contraction of gene families (see Methods and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig6">Extended Data Fig. 2</a>). MRCA, most recent common ancestor. The number in parentheses is the number of gene families in the MRCA as estimated by CAFÃ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="De Bie, T ., Cristianini, N ., Demuth, J. P &amp; Hahn, M. W. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics 22, 1269â1271 (2006)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1231">11</a></sup>.</p><p>
<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM2">PowerPoint slide</a>
</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature23897/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Distributions of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (<i>K</i><sub>S</sub>) (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 2.1</a>) for paralogous <i>A. shenzhenica</i> genes showed a clear peak at <i>K</i><sub>S</sub>âââ1 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 4</a>). Similar peaks at <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> values of 0.7 to 1.1 were identified in 11 other orchids, covering all 5 orchid subfamilies (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). These peaks might reflect multiple independent whole-genome duplication (WGD) events across orchid sublineages or, more parsimoniously, a single WGD event shared by all (extant) orchids. Comparisons of orchid paralogue <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> distributions with <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> distributions of orthologues between orchid species, and between orchids and <i>Asparagus officinalis</i> (asparagus, Asparagaceae, a sister family to Orchidaceae in the order Asparagales) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 2.1</a>) indicated that the WGD signatures are not shared with non-orchid Asparagales. Absolute phylogenomic dating<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Vanneste, K., Baele, G., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Analysis of 41 plant genomes supports a wave of successful genome duplications in association with the Cretaceous-Paleogene boundary. Genome Res. 24, 1334â1347 (2014)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1295">12</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig9">Extended Data Fig. 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 2.1</a>) revealed that the WGDs and the earliest diversification of extant orchid lineages occurred relatively close together in time, supporting the possibility of a single WGD event in the most recent common ancestor of extant orchids.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="KS and co-linearity analysis of the A. shenzhenica WGD."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> and co-linearity analysis of the <i>A. shenzhenica</i> WGD.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature23897/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature23897_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1372" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Distribution of <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> for the one-to-one <i>P. equestris</i>â<i>D. catenatum</i>, <i>A. shenzhenica</i>â<i>D. catenatum</i>, <i>A. shenzhenica</i>â<i>P. equestris</i> and <i>A. shenzhenica</i>â<i>A. officinalis</i> orthologues (filled grey curves and left-hand <i>y</i>-axis). Distribution of <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> for duplicated anchors found in co-linear regions of <i>A. shenzhenica</i> (green lines), <i>D. catenatum</i> (red lines) and <i>P. equestris</i> (blue lines). The filled grey curves and dashed coloured lines are actual data points from the distributions; the solid coloured lines are kernel density estimates (KDE) of the anchor-pair (duplicated genes found in co-linear regions) data scaled to match the corresponding dashed lines. All anchor-pair data are scaled up Ã15 (right-hand <i>y</i>-axis) compared to the orthologue data. <b>b</b>, Syntenic dot plot of the self-comparison of <i>A. shenzhenica</i>. Only co-linear segments with at least 15 anchor pairs are shown. The sections on each scaffold with co-linear segments are shown in grey. The red bars below the dot plot illustrate the duplication depths (the number of connected co-linear segments overlapping at each position; see Methods). The co-linear regions in green indicate the four co-linear segments that have a common orthologous co-linear segment in <i>A. trichopoda</i> as shown in (<b>c</b>). <b>c</b>, Co-linear alignment of <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and <i>A. trichopoda</i>. The colours of genes in the alignment indicate gene orientation, with blue for forward strands and green for reverse strands. The grey links connect orthologues between <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and <i>A. trichopoda</i>. Scf86, scaffold00086 of the <i>A. trichopoda</i> genome (v1.0).</p><p>
<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM3">PowerPoint slide</a>
</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature23897/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Intragenomic co-linearity and synteny analysis identified two WGD events in <i>A. shenzhenica</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 2.2</a>). Co-linearity and synteny analyses between <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and <i>Amborella trichopoda</i>, and between <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, also support at least two WGDs in <i>A. shenzhenica</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Figs 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">5</a>); for example, four paralogous segments in <i>A. shenzhenica</i> corresponded to one orthologous region in <i>A. trichopoda</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>). Detailed genome comparisons of <i>A. shenzhenica</i> with <i>Ananas comosus</i> (pineapple) and <i>A. officinalis</i> revealed a specific 4:4 co-linearity pattern (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig10">Extended Data Fig. 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Figs 6â8</a>) that is consistent with the two monocot WGDs proposed for <i>A. comosus</i>, indicating that all three species possess a similar evolutionary history with regard to WGDs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 2.2</a>). Together, these patterns of co-linearity suggest that the older of the two WGDs evident in <i>A. shenzhenica</i> is likely to be shared with <i>A. comosus</i> and <i>A. officinalis</i> (representing the Ï WGD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Jiao, Y., Li, J., Tang, H. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Integrated syntenic and phylogenomic analyses reveal an ancient genome duplication in monocots. Plant Cell 26, 2792â2802 (2014)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1502">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. 47, 1435â1442 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1505">14</a></sup> shared by most monocots), and corroborate the idea that the younger WGD represents an independent event, specific to the Orchidaceae lineage. Analyses of gene trees that contained at least one paralogue pair from co-linear regions from one of the three orchid genomes placed the younger <i>A. shenzhenica</i> WGD and the <i>P. equestris</i> and <i>D. catenatum</i> WGDs on the orchid stem branch, and also provided additional evidence for the monocot Ï WGD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Jiao, Y., Li, J., Tang, H. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Integrated syntenic and phylogenomic analyses reveal an ancient genome duplication in monocots. Plant Cell 26, 2792â2802 (2014)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1518">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. 47, 1435â1442 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1521">14</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig3">Fig. 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 2.3</a>). We therefore find strong support for a WGD event shared by all extant orchids, which is likely to be only slightly older than their earliest divergence and might be correlated with orchid diversification. In addition, as observed for many other plant lineages, this orchid-specific WGD might be associated with the Cretaceous/Palaeogene boundary<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Van de Peer, Y., Mizrachi, E. &amp; Marchal, K. The evolutionary significance of polyploidy. Nat. Rev. Genet. 18, 411â424 (2017)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1532">15</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Phylogenomic analysis of orchid WGD events."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Phylogenomic analysis of orchid WGD events.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature23897/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature23897/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature23897_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature23897/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature23897_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="619" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>The numbers on the branches of the species tree indicate the number of gene families with one or more anchor pairs from at least one of the three orchids with genomes that coalesced on the respective branch (top), as well as the individual contributions of anchor pairs from the three orchids (bottom; A, <i>A. shenzhenica</i>; D, <i>D. catenatum</i>; P, <i>P. equestris</i>). The two WGD events identified are depicted by stars. Species with published genomes are in bold. All the duplication events have bootstrap values over 80% (see Methods; for results for bootstrap values over 50% see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>).</p><p>
<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM4">PowerPoint slide</a>
</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature23897/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p><i>Apostasia</i> presents a number of characters that are plesiomorphic in orchids, such as an actinomorphic perianth with an undifferentiated labellum, a gynostemium with partially fused androecium and gynoecium, pollen that is not aggregated into pollinia, and underground roots for terrestrial growth<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Roberts, D. L. &amp; Dixon, K. W. Orchids. Curr. Biol. 18, R325âR329 (2008)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1579">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Kocyan, A., Qiu, Y.-L., Endress, P. K. &amp; Conti, E. A phylogenetic analysis of Apostasioideae (Orchidaceae) based on ITS, trnL-F and matK sequences. Plant Syst. Evol. 247, 203â213 (2004)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1582">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Dressler, R. L. Phylogeny and Classification of the Orchid Family (Discorides, 1993)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1585">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kocyan, A. &amp; Endress, P. K. Floral structure and development of Apostasia and Neuwiedia (Apostasioideae) and their relationships to other Orchidaceae. Int. J. Plant Sci. 162, 847â867 (2001)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1588">7</a></sup>. The <i>A. shenzhenica</i> genome contains 36 putative functional MADS-box genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Tab1">Table 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>), 27 of which are type II MADS-box genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Tab1">Table 1</a>). Two type II MADS-box classes appear to be reduced: <i>A. shenzhenica</i> seems to have fewer genes in the B-AP3 (two members) and E classes (three members) than <i>P. equestris</i> (four B-AP3 and six E-class members) and <i>D. catenatum</i> (four B-AP3 and five E-class members) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>). Previous studies have shown that expanded B-AP3 and E classes with members that have different expression patterns in floral organs are associated with the innovation of the unique labellum and gynostemium in orchids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Cai, J. et al. The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. Nat. Genet. 47, 65â72 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1620">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Tsai, W. C., Kuoh, C. S., Chuang, M. H., Chen, W. H. &amp; Chen, H. H. Four DEF-like MADS box genes displayed distinct floral morphogenetic roles in Phalaenopsis orchid. Plant Cell Physiol. 45, 831â844 (2004)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1623">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Pan, Z. J. et al. Flower development of Phalaenopsis orchid involves functionally divergent SEPALLATA-like genes. New Phytol. 202, 1024â1042 (2014)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1626">17</a></sup>, and that duplicated <i>B-AP3</i> genes are responsible for the modularization of the perianth of orchid flowers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="MondragÃ³n-Palomino, M. &amp; Theissen, G. Why are orchid flowers so diverse? Reduction of evolutionary constraints by paralogues of class B floral homeotic genes. Ann. Bot. 104, 583â594 (2009)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d44148e1634">18</a></sup>. We identified <i>B-AP3</i> genes from the transcriptomes of species of each of the orchid subfamilies and the B-class MADS-box genes from the floral transcriptome data of <i>Molineria capitulata</i>, a member of Hypoxidaceae that possesses a flower with petaloid tepals and powdery pollen (similar to that found in <i>Apostasia</i>). We found one member in each of the two B-AP3 subclades for both <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and <i>M. capitulata</i>, but one or two members in each B-AP3 subclade for the other orchids (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig11">Extended Data Fig. 7</a>). All these <i>B-AP3</i> genes are highly expressed in flower buds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig11">Extended Data Fig. 7</a>). These similarities suggest that the lower gene numbers in MADS-box B-AP3 and E classes in <i>Apostasia</i> represent an ancestral state, responsible for producing the plesiomorphic flower with an undifferentiated labellum and partially fused gynostemium. The B-AP3 and E classes may have expanded independently only in the non-apostasioid orchids or, alternatively, in the common ancestor of all extant orchids, possibly as a result of the shared orchid WGD, with subsequent loss of paralogous genes in <i>Apostasia</i> causing reversion to the ancestral state. The <i>B-AP3</i> gene tree topology and some evidence from co-linearity analysis of orchid <i>B-AP3</i> genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>) suggest the latter. We hypothesize that differential paralogue retention and subsequent sub- and neo-functionalization of B-AP3 and E-class members resulted in the derived labellum found in other orchids (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 MADS-box genes in the <i>A. shenzhenica</i>, <i>P. equestris</i>, <i>D. catenatum</i>, <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>O. sativa</i> genomes</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nature23897/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="MADS-box genes involved in orchid morphological evolution."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: MADS-box genes involved in orchid morphological evolution.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature23897/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature23897/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature23897_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature23897/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature23897_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="410" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Phylogenetic analysis of MADS-box genes among <i>A. shenzhenica</i>, <i>P. equestris</i>, <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>Arabidopsis</i>. The B-AP3 and E-class, MIKC*, MÎ², and AGL12 and ANR1 subclades are marked by purple, orange, green and blue shading, respectively. <b>b</b>, <i>A. shenzhenica</i>, with fewer B-AP3 class and E class MADS-box genes, keeps an undifferentiated labellum and partially fused gynostemium, while <i>P. equestris</i>, with more B-AP3 class and E class MADS-box genes, develops the specialized labellum and column (in red). <b>c</b>, Loss of the P-subclade genes of MIKC* in <i>P. equestris</i> is likely to be related to the evolution of pollinia. <b>d</b>, The failed development of endosperm in orchids might be related to the missing type I MÎ² MADS-box genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig13">Extended Data Fig. 9</a>). <b>e</b>, <i>A. shenzhenica</i>, containing the <i>AGL12</i> gene and expanded ANR1 genes, is a terrestrial orchid, while epiphytic orchids, such as <i>P. equestris</i>, have lost the <i>AGL12</i> gene and some ANR1 genes.</p><p>
<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM5">PowerPoint slide</a>
</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature23897/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The packaging of pollen grains into a compact unit known as the pollinium, specialized for transfer as a unit by pollinating vectors, was a key innovation in the evolutionary history of Orchidaceae and may have played a role in promoting the tremendous radiation of the group<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Johnson, S. D. &amp; Edwards, T. J. The structure and function of orchid pollinaria. Plant Syst. Evol. 222, 243â269 (2000)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2617">19</a></sup>. In seed plants, the P- and S-subclades of MIKC*-type genes are major regulators of male gametophytic development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Liu, Y. et al. Functional conservation of MIKC*-Type MADS box genes in Arabidopsis and rice pollen maturation. Plant Cell 25, 1288â1303 (2013)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2621">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Kwantes, M., Liebsch, D. &amp; Verelst, W. How MIKC* MADS-box genes originated and evidence for their conserved function throughout the evolution of vascular plant gametophytes. Mol. Biol. Evol. 29, 293â302 (2012)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2624">21</a></sup>. The P-subclade, however, is absent in all orchids except <i>A. shenzhenica</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig12">Extended Data Fig. 8</a>). Gene expression analysis showed that, in orchids and <i>M. capitulata</i>, MIKC*-type genes are expressed in the pollinia or pollen, suggesting they play roles in its development (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig13">Extended Data Fig. 9</a>). Although most orchids have a pollinium, <i>Apostasia</i> has scattered pollen, similar to <i>M. capitulata</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i> (rice), and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>. Therefore, we propose that the loss of the P-subclade members of MIKC*-type genes is related to the evolution of the pollinium (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig4">Fig. 4a, c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 3</a>).</p><p>The reduction of seed volume and content to an absolute minimum is a pivotal aspect of Orchidaceae evolution: in all orchid species, endosperm is absent from the seed. Type I MADS-box genes are important for the initiation of endosperm development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Masiero, S., Colombo, L., Grini, P. E., Schnittger, A. &amp; Kater, M. M. The emerging importance of type I MADS box transcription factors for plant reproduction. Plant Cell 23, 865â872 (2011)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2663">22</a></sup>, and transcripts of type I MÎ± and MÎ³ MADS-box genes were found in developing seeds of <i>A. shenzhenica</i>, <i>P. equestris</i>, and <i>M. capitulata</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig14">Extended Data Fig. 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). Notably, the three orchid genomes do not contain any type I MÎ² MADS-box genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>), which are found in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> (poplar), <i>O. sativa</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Tab1">Table 1</a>), and in <i>M. capitulata</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). The lack of endosperm in orchids might therefore be related to the missing type I MÎ² MADS-box genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig4">Fig. 4d</a>).</p><p>Orchids are one of very few flowering plant lineages that have been able to successfully colonize epiphytic or lithophytic niches, clinging to trees or rocks and growing in dry conditions using crassulacean acid metabolism<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Givnish, T. J. et al. Orchid phylogenomics and multiple drivers of their extraordinary diversification. Proc. R. Soc. B 282, 1553 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2714">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Cai, J. et al. The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. Nat. Genet. 47, 65â72 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2717">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zhang, G.-Q. et al. The Dendrobium catenatum Lindl. genome sequence provides insights into polysaccharide synthase, floral development and adaptive evolution. Sci. Rep. 6, 19029 (2016)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2720">10</a></sup>. The roots of epiphytic orchids, such as <i>Phalaenopsis</i> and <i>Dendrobium</i>, are extremely specialized and differ from the roots of terrestrial orchids such as <i>Apostasia</i>. These aerial roots develop the velamen radicum, a spongy epidermis that traps the nutrient-rich flush during rainfall, representing an important adaptation of epiphytic orchids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Zotz, G. &amp; Winkler, U. Aerial roots of epiphytic orchids: the velamen radicum and its role in water and nutrient uptake. Oecologia 171, 733â741 (2013)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2733">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Chomicki, G. et al. The velamen protects photosynthetic orchid roots against UV-B damage, and a large dated phylogeny implies multiple gains and losses of this function during the Cenozoic. New Phytol. 205, 1330â1341 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2736">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Gravendeel, B., Smithson, A., Slik, F. J. W. &amp; Schuiteman, A. Epiphytism and pollinator specialization: drivers for orchid diversity? Phil. Trans. R. Soc. Lond. B 359, 1523â1535 (2004)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2739">25</a></sup>. The <i>Arabidopsis AGL12</i> gene is involved in root cell differentiation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Tapia-LÃ³pez, R. et al. An AGAMOUS-related MADS-box gene, XAL1 (AGL12), regulates root meristem cell proliferation and flowering transition in Arabidopsis. Plant Physiol. 146, 1182â1192 (2008)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2747">26</a></sup>. <i>A. shenzhenica</i> contains one AGL12 clade gene, as do <i>Arabidopsis</i> and rice. In addition, we found transcripts similar to <i>AGL12</i> in <i>M. capitulata</i>. In both <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and <i>M. capitulata</i>, these genes are highly expressed in root tissue (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 14</a>). Notably, we did not find similar genes in epiphytic orchids, suggesting that the loss of these gene(s) may be involved in losing the ability to develop true roots for terrestrial growth (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig4">Fig. 4e</a>). <i>Utricularia gibba</i>, an asterid in the order Lamiales (only distantly related to the orchids) that lacks true roots, also lacks these AGL12 clade or similar genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Ibarra-Laclette, E. et al. Architecture and evolution of a minute plant genome. Nature 498, 94â98 (2013)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2779">27</a></sup>. The <i>Arabidopsis ANR1</i> gene is a key gene involved in regulating lateral root development in response to external nitrate supply<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Zhang, H. &amp; Forde, B. G. An Arabidopsis MADS box gene that controls nutrient-induced changes in root architecture. Science 279, 407â409 (1998)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2787">28</a></sup>. We found that the MADS-box gene subfamily ANR1 is probably reduced in <i>P. equestris</i> (two members) and <i>D. catenatum</i> (three members), compared with four members in <i>A. shenzhenica</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>): this is consistent with no development of lateral (aerial) roots in epiphytic orchids.</p><p>In conclusion, the genome sequence of <i>A. shenzhenica</i>, an orchid belonging to a small clade that is sister to the rest of Orchidaceae, provides a reference for studying orchid evolution, revealing clear evidence of an ancient WGD shared by all orchids, facilitating reconstruction of the ancestral orchid gene toolkit, and providing insights into many orchid-specific features such as the development of the labellum and gynostemium, pollinia, and seeds without endosperm, as well as the evolution of epiphytism.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>No statistical methods were used to predetermine sample size.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sample preparation and sequencing</h3><p>For genome sequencing, we collected leaves, stems, and flowers from wild <i>A. shenzhenica</i>, a self-pollinating species found in southeast China<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Chen, L. J. &amp; Liu, Z. J. Apostasia shenzhenica: a new species of Apostasioideae (Orchidaceae) from China. Plant Science Journal 29, 38â41 (2011)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2827">4</a></sup> that has a karyotype of 2<i>N</i>â=â2Xâ=â68 with uniform small chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 16</a>). We extracted genomic DNA using a modified cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB) protocol. Sequencing libraries with insert sizes ranging from 180 bp to 20âkb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 1</a>) were constructed using a library construction kit (Illumina). These libraries were then sequenced using an Illumina HiSeq 2000 platform. The 80.02-Gb raw reads generated were filtered according to sequencing quality, the presence of adaptor contamination, and duplication. Only high-quality reads were used for genome assembly.</p><p>Total RNA was extracted from this studyâs samples using the RNAprep Pure Plant Kit and genomic DNA contamination was removed using RNase-Free DNase I (both from Tiangen). The integrity of RNA was evaluated on a 1.0% agarose gel stained with ethidium bromide (EB), and its quality and quantity were assessed using a NanoPhotometer spectrophotometer (IMPLEN) and an Agilent 2100 Bioanalyzer (Agilent Technologies). As the RNA integrity number (RIN) was greater than 7.0 for all samples, they were used in cDNA library construction and Illumina sequencing, which was completed by Beijing Novogene Bioinformatics Technology Co., Ltd. The cDNA library was constructed using the NEBNext Ultra RNA Library Prep Kit for Illumina (NEB) and 3âÎ¼g RNA per sample, following the manufacturerâs recommendations. The PCR products obtained were purified (AMPure XP system) and library quality was assessed on the Agilent Bioanalyzer 2100 system. Library preparations were sequenced on an Illumina Hiseq 2000 platform, generating 100-bp paired-end reads.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome size estimation and preliminary assembly</h3><p>The genome size of species in Apostasioideae is between 0.38 pg and 5.96 pg<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="JersÃ¡kovÃ¡, J. et al. Genome size variation in Orchidaceae subfamily Apostasioideae: filling the phylogenetic gap. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 172, 95â105 (2013)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2851">31</a></sup>, which is relatively small compared to that of other subfamilies (ranging from 0.38 pg to 55.4 pg)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Leitch, I. J. et al. Genome size diversity in orchids: consequences and evolution. Ann. Bot. 104, 469â481 (2009)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2855">32</a></sup>. To estimate the genome size of <i>A. shenzhenica</i>, we used reads from paired-end libraries to determine the distribution of <i>K</i>-mer values. According to the LanderâWaterman theory<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Lander, E. S. &amp; Waterman, M. S. Genomic mapping by fingerprinting random clones: a mathematical analysis. Genomics 2, 231â239 (1988)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2865">33</a></sup>, genome size can be determined by the total number of <i>K</i>-mers divided by the peak value of the <i>K</i>-mer distribution. Given only one peak in the <i>K</i>-mer distribution, we found that <i>A. shenzhenica</i> has no heterozygosity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 17</a>). With the peak at the expected <i>K</i>-mer depth and the formula genome sizeâ=âtotal <i>K</i>-mer/expected <i>K-</i>mer depth, the size of the haploid genome was estimated to be 471.0âMb (haploid). We used ALLPATHS-LG software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Butler, J. et al. ALLPATHS: de novo assembly of whole-genome shotgun microreads. Genome Res. 18, 810â820 (2008)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2895">34</a></sup> and obtained a preliminary assembly of <i>A. shenzhenica</i> with a scaffold N50 size of 1.196 Mb and corresponding contig N50 size of 30.1âKb.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">PacBio library construction and sequencing and filling gaps</h3><p>The preliminary assembly of <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and the previous published genome assemblies of <i>P. equestris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Cai, J. et al. The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. Nat. Genet. 47, 65â72 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2915">9</a></sup> and <i>D. catenatum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zhang, G.-Q. et al. The Dendrobium catenatum Lindl. genome sequence provides insights into polysaccharide synthase, floral development and adaptive evolution. Sci. Rep. 6, 19029 (2016)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2921">10</a></sup> were improved using PacBio and 10X Genomics Linked-Reads.</p><p>Genomic DNA was isolated from the leaves of <i>A. shenzhenica</i>, <i>P. equestris</i> and <i>D. catenatum</i>. For a 20-kb insert size library, at least 20âÎ¼g of sheared DNA was required. SMRTbell template preparation involved DNA concentration, damage repair, end repair, ligation of hairpin adapters, and template purification, and used AMPure PB Magnetic Beads. Finally, the sequencing primer was annealed and sequencing polymerase was bound to SMRTbell template. The instructions specified as calculated by the RS Remote software were followed. We carried out 20-kb single-molecule real-time DNA sequencing by PacBio and sequenced the DNA library on the PacBio RS II platform, yielding about 5.44 Gb (<i>A. shenzhenica</i>), 10.54 Gb (<i>P. equestris</i>) and 11.06 Gb (<i>D. catenatum</i>) PacBio data (read qualityââ¥â0.80, mean read length of <i>A. shenzhenica</i> â¥â7 Kb, of <i>P. equestris</i> and <i>D. catenatum</i> â¥â10 Kb) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 2</a>).</p><p>We used PBjelly software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="English, A. C. et al. Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with Pacific Biosciences RS long-read sequencing technology. PLoS One 7, e47768 (2012)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2962">35</a></sup> to fill gaps with PacBio data. The options were â&lt;blasr&gt;-minMatch 8 -sdpTupleSize 8 -minPctIdentity 75 -bestn 1 -nCandidates 10 -maxScore -500 -nproc 10 -noSplitSubreads&lt;/blasr&gt;â for the protocol.xml file. Then, we used Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS One 9, e112963 (2014)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2966">36</a></sup> with default settings to correct assembled errors. For the input BAM file, we used BWA to align all the Illumina short reads to the assembly and SAMTOOLS to sort and index the BAM file.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">10X Genomics library construction, sequencing, and extending scaffolds</h3><p>DNA sample preparation, indexing, and barcoding were done using the GemCode Instrument from 10X Genomics. About 0.7âng input DNA with 50âkb length was used for GEM reaction procedure during PCR, and 16-bp barcodes were introduced into droplets. Then, the droplets were fractured following the purifying of the intermediate DNA library. Next, we sheared DNA into 500 bp for constructing libraries, which were finally sequenced on the Illumina HiseqXTen<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Zheng, G. X. et al. Haplotyping germline and cancer genomes with high-throughput linked-read sequencing. Nat. Biotechnol. 34, 303â311 (2016)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2979">37</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 3</a>).</p><p>We used BWA mem to align the 10X Genomics data to the filled gaps assembly using default settings. Then, we used fragScaff<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Adey, A. et al. In vitro, long-range sequence information for de novo genome assembly via transposase contiguity. Genome Res. 24, 2041â2049 (2014)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d44148e2989">38</a></sup> for scaffolding. The options were as follows: <i>A. shenzhenica</i> (stages1 â-m 3000 -q 30â; stages2 â-C 2â; stages3 â-j 1.25 -u 2â), <i>D. catenatum</i> (stages1 â-m 3000 -q 30â; stages2 â-C 1â; stages3 â-j 2 -u 2â) and <i>P. equestris</i> (stages1 â-m 3000 -q 30â; stages2 â-C 1â; stages3 â-j 2 -u 2â)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Mostovoy, Y. et al. A hybrid approach for de novo human genome sequence assembly and phasing. Nat. Methods 13, 587â590 (2016)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3002">39</a></sup>.</p><p>The total length of the final assembly for <i>A. shenzhenica</i> was 349âMb with a scaffold N50 size of 3.029âMb and corresponding contig N50 size of 80.1âKb. (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 4</a>). For the two previously published orchid genomes of <i>P. equestris</i> and <i>D. catenatum</i>, the scaffold N50 size as well as the completeness (see below) improved considerably: for <i>P. equestris</i>, the scaffold N50 size increased from 359.12âKb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Cai, J. et al. The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. Nat. Genet. 47, 65â72 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3025">9</a></sup> to 1.217âMb and the corresponding contig N50 size from 20.56âKb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Cai, J. et al. The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. Nat. Genet. 47, 65â72 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3029">9</a></sup> to 45.79âKb, while for <i>D. catenatum</i> the scaffold N50 size increased from 391.46âKb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zhang, G.-Q. et al. The Dendrobium catenatum Lindl. genome sequence provides insights into polysaccharide synthase, floral development and adaptive evolution. Sci. Rep. 6, 19029 (2016)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3036">10</a></sup> to 1.055âMb, and the corresponding contig N50 size from 33.1âKb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zhang, G.-Q. et al. The Dendrobium catenatum Lindl. genome sequence provides insights into polysaccharide synthase, floral development and adaptive evolution. Sci. Rep. 6, 19029 (2016)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3040">10</a></sup> up to 51.7âKb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 7</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Repeat prediction</h3><p>A total of 146.65âMb of repetitive elements occupying more than 42.05% of the <i>A. shenzhenica</i> genome were annotated using a combination of structural information and homology prediction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zhang, G.-Q. et al. The Dendrobium catenatum Lindl. genome sequence provides insights into polysaccharide synthase, floral development and adaptive evolution. Sci. Rep. 6, 19029 (2016)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3058">10</a></sup>. Retrotransposable elements, known to be the dominant form of repeats in angiosperm genomes, constituted a large part of the <i>A. shenzhenica</i> genome and included the most abundant subtypes, such as LTR/Copia (4.97%), LTR/Gypsy (11.84%), LINE/L1 (2.78%) and LINE/RTE-BovB (9.32%), among others. In addition, the percentage of <i>de novo</i> predicted repeats was notably larger than that obtained for homologous repeats based on Repbase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Bao, W., Kojima, K. K. &amp; Kohany, O. Repbase Update, a database of repetitive elements in eukaryotic genomes. Mob. DNA 6, 11 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3068">40</a></sup>, indicating that <i>A. shenzhenica</i> has multiple unique repeats compared with other sequenced plant species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 9</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Gene and non-coding RNA prediction</h3><p>MAKER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Holt, C. &amp; Yandell, M. MAKER2: an annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 491 (2011)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3087">41</a></sup> was used to generate a consensus gene set based on <i>de novo</i> predictions from AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Keller, O., Kollmar, M., Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. A novel hybrid gene prediction method employing protein multiple sequence alignments. Bioinformatics 27, 757â763 (2011)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3094">42</a></sup> and GlimmerHMM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878â2879 (2004)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3098">43</a></sup>, homology annotation with the universal single-copy genes from CEGMA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061â1067 (2007)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3102">44</a></sup> and the genes from <i>Arabidopsis</i> (TAIR10) and another four sequenced monocots (<i>O. sativa</i>, <i>P. equestris</i>, <i>S. bicolor</i> and <i>Zea mays</i>) using exonerate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Slater, G. S. &amp; Birney, E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinformatics 6, 31 (2005)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3122">45</a></sup>, and RNA-seq prediction by Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protocols 7, 562â578 (2012)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3127">46</a></sup> and Tophat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Kim, D. et al. TopHat2: accurate alignment of transcriptomes in the presence of insertions, deletions and gene fusions. Genome Biol. 14, R36 (2013)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3131">47</a></sup>. These results were integrated into a final set of protein-coding genes for annotation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 5</a>). Using the same annotation pipeline as for <i>A. shenzhenica</i>, 29,545 and 29,257 protein-coding genes were predicted for <i>P. equestris</i> and <i>D. catenatum</i>, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 7</a>). <i>A. shenzhenica</i> was found to have a greater average gene length (here we considered the start and stop codons as the two boundaries for a gene) than most other sequenced plants, but this length was similar to that of <i>P. equestris</i> and <i>D. catenatum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 18</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 10</a>), in both of which this is due to a long average intron length<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Cai, J. et al. The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. Nat. Genet. 47, 65â72 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3167">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zhang, G.-Q. et al. The Dendrobium catenatum Lindl. genome sequence provides insights into polysaccharide synthase, floral development and adaptive evolution. Sci. Rep. 6, 19029 (2016)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3170">10</a></sup>.</p><p>We then generated functional assignments of the <i>A. shenzhenica</i> genes with BLAST (version 2.2.28+) by aligning their protein-coding regions to sequences in public protein databases, including KEGG (59.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Ogata, H. et al. KEGG: Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 29â34 (1999)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3180">48</a></sup>, SwissProt (release 2013_06)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Bairoch, A. &amp; Apweiler, R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 45â48 (2000)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3184">49</a></sup>, TrEMBL (release 2013_06)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Boeckmann, B. et al. The SWISS-PROT protein knowledgebase and its supplement TrEMBL in 2003. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 365â370 (2003)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3188">50</a></sup> and NCBI non-redundant protein database (20150617), and InterProScan (v5.11-51.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Zdobnov, E. M. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterProScan--an integration platform for the signature-recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics 17, 847â848 (2001)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3192">51</a></sup> was also used to provide functional annotation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 11</a>). We were able to generate functional assignments for 84.2% of the <i>A. shenzhenica</i> genes from at least one of the public protein databases (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 11</a>).</p><p>The tRNA genes were searched by tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955â964 (1997)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3209">52</a></sup>. For rRNA identification, we downloaded the <i>Arabidopsis</i> rRNA sequences from NCBI and aligned them with the <i>A. shenzhenica</i> genome to identify possible rRNAs. Additionally, other types of non-coding RNAs, including miRNA and snRNA, were identified by using INFERNAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Nawrocki, E. P., Kolbe, D. L. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics 25, 1335â1337 (2009)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3219">53</a></sup> to search from the Rfam database.
