In the end, we identified 43 microRNAs, 203 transfer RNAs, 452 ribosomal RNAs and 93 small nuclear RNAs in the <i>A. shenzhenica</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 12</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Transcriptome assembly</h3><p>Before assembly, we got high-quality reads by removing adaptor sequences and filtered low-quality reads by using TRIMMOMATIC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114â2120 (2014)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3238">54</a></sup> from raw reads with parameters: ILLUMINACLIP:path/adaptor:2:30:10 LEADING:5 TRAILING:5 SLIDINGWINDOW:4:15 MINLEN:36. The resulting high-quality reads were <i>de novo</i> assembled and annotated with the TRINITY program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Haas, B. J. et al. De novo transcript sequence reconstruction from RNA-seq using the Trinity platform for reference generation and analysis. Nat. Protocols 8, 1494â1512 (2013)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3245">55</a></sup>. The commands and parameters used for running TRINITY were as follows: Trinity âseqType fq âJM 200G âleft sample_1.fq âright sample_2.fq ânormalize_by_read_set âCPU 32 âoutput sample âmin_kmer_cov 2. Protein sequences and coding sequences of transcripts were predicted using TransDecoder (<a href="http://transdecoder.github.io">http://transdecoder.github.io</a>), a software tool that identifies likely coding sequences from transcript sequences and compares the translated coding sequences with the PFAM domain database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Haas, B. J. et al. De novo transcript sequence reconstruction from RNA-seq using the Trinity platform for reference generation and analysis. Nat. Protocols 8, 1494â1512 (2013)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3256">55</a></sup>. For genes with more than one transcript, the longest one was used to calculate transcript abundance and coverage. Transcript abundance level was normalized using the fragments per kilobase per million mapped reads (FPKM) method, and FPKM values were computed as proposed by Mortazavi <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Mortazavi, A., Williams, B. A., McCue, K., Schaeffer, L. &amp; Wold, B. Mapping and quantifying mammalian transcriptomes by RNA-Seq. Nat. Methods 5, 621â628 (2008)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3264">56</a></sup>.</p><p>Transcriptomes of <i>Agave deserti</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Gross, S. M. et al. De novo transcriptome assembly of drought tolerant CAM plants, Agave deserti and Agave tequilana. BMC Genomics 14, 563 (2013)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3273">57</a></sup> and <i>Allium cepa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Duangjit, J., Bohanec, B., Chan, A. P., Town, C. D. &amp; Havey, M. J. Transcriptome sequencing to produce SNP-based genetic maps of onion. Theor. Appl. Genet. 126, 2093â2101 (2013)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3279">58</a></sup> were downloaded from Dryad (h5t68) and NCBI (PRJNA175446), respectively. We removed the redundant unigenes in <i>A. cepa</i> by CD-HIT-EST with 99% identity and used TransDecoder to predict proteins with default parameters.</p><p>We carried out BLASTP (<i>E</i> value &lt;1âÃâ10<sup>â3</sup>) to search the best hits for the proteins predicted in the transcriptomes against a customized database, built with proteins from the genomes of <i>A. shenzhenica</i>, <i>P. equestris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Cai, J. et al. The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. Nat. Genet. 47, 65â72 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3299">9</a></sup>, <i>D. catenatum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zhang, G.-Q. et al. The Dendrobium catenatum Lindl. genome sequence provides insights into polysaccharide synthase, floral development and adaptive evolution. Sci. Rep. 6, 19029 (2016)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3306">10</a></sup>, and <i>A. officinalis</i> (GenBank accession number GCF_001876935.1) as well as public databases, such as NCBI Plant RefSeq (release 80), Ensembl (release 77), Ensembl Metazoa (release 24), Ensembl Fungi (release 24), and Ensembl Protists (release 24). Only plant-homologous proteins were retained in the transcriptomes to eliminate the effects of genes derived from commensal organisms, laboratory contaminants, and artefacts resulting from incorrect assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 13</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Gene family identification</h3><p>We downloaded genome and annotation data of <i>A. trichopoda</i> (<a href="http://amborella.huck.psu.edu">http://amborella.huck.psu.edu</a>, version1.0), <i>A. comosus</i> (GenBank accession number GCF_001540865.1), <i>A. thaliana</i> (TAIR 10), <i>A. officinalis</i> (GenBank accession number GCF_001876935.1), <i>B. distachyon</i> (purple false brome; Phytozome v9.0), <i>M. acuminata</i> (<a href="http://ensemblgenomes.org">http://ensemblgenomes.org</a>, release-21), <i>O. sativa</i> (Nipponbare, IRGSP-1.0), <i>P. dactylifera</i> (<a href="https://qatar-weill.cornell.edu/research-labs-and-programs/date-palm-research-program/date-palm-genome-data">http://qatar-weill.cornell.edu/research/datepalmGenome</a>), <i>P. trichocarpa</i> (<a href="http://ensemblgenomes.org">http://ensemblgenomes.org</a>, release-21), <i>S. bicolor</i> (sorghum; Phytozome v9.0), <i>S. polyrhiza</i> (common duckweed; <a href="http://spirodelagenome.org/">http://www.spirodelagenome.org</a>), and <i>V. vinifera</i> (Phytozome v9.0). We chose the longest transcript to represent each gene and removed gene models with open reading frames shorter than 150 bp. Gene family clustering was performed using OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J., Jr &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3398">59</a></sup> based on the set of 21,841 predicted genes of <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and the protein sets of the above ten other monocots, three dicots and the outgroup <i>A. trichopoda.</i> This analysis yielded 11,995 gene families in <i>A. shenzhenica</i> containing 18,268 predicted genes (83.6% of the total genes identified; orthologous genes in the 15 sequenced plant species are shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 19</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 14</a>) (see also <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Note 1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Phylogenetic tree construction and phylogenomic dating</h3><p>We constructed a phylogenetic tree based on a concatenated sequence alignment of 439 single-copy gene families from <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and the 14 other plant species using MrBayes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Huelsenbeck, J. P. &amp; Ronquist, F. MRBAYES: Bayesian inference of phylogenetic trees. Bioinformatics 17, 754â755 (2001)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3432">60</a></sup> software with GTR+Î model (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). For the phylogenetic analysis incorporating ten additional transcriptome species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig6">Extended Data Fig. 2</a>), we first picked up the genes of <i>A. shenzhenica</i>, <i>D. catenatum</i>, and <i>P. equestris</i> in the single-copy gene families as seed genes, and then made a BLASTP alignment between the transcriptome unigenes and the seed sequences. For one single-copy family, if the three seed genes all had the identical best-hit to a unigene, this gene was identified as the orthologous gene to the gene family. With this method we found 132 single-copy gene families of the total 25 species, then constructed the phylogenetic tree based on a concatenated sequence alignment of them using PhyML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Guindon, S. et al. New algorithms and methods to estimate maximum-likelihood phylogenies: assessing the performance of PhyML 3.0. Syst. Biol. 59, 307â321 (2010)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3452">61</a></sup> with GTR+Î model. Divergence times were estimated by PAML MCMCTREE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3456">62</a></sup>. The Markov chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) process was run for 1,500,000 iterations with a sample frequency of 150 after a burn-in of 500,000 iterations. Other parameters used the default settings of MCMCTREE. Two independent runs were performed to check convergence. The following constraints were used for time calibrations: (i) the <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>B. distachyon</i> divergence time (40â54 million years ago (Ma))<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763â768 (2010)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3467">63</a></sup>; (ii) the <i>P. trichocarpa</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> divergence time (100â120 Ma)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3477">64</a></sup>; (iii) the monocot and eudicot divergence time with a lower boundary of 130 Ma<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3481">65</a></sup>; and (iv) 200 Ma as the upper boundary for the earliest-diverging angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="MagallÃ³n, S., Hilu, K. W. &amp; Quandt, D. Land plant evolutionary timeline: gene effects are secondary to fossil constraints in relaxed clock estimation of age and substitution rates. Am. J. Bot. 100, 556â573 (2013)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3485">66</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Identification of WGD events in <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and phylogenomic analyses</h3><p><i>K</i><sub>S</sub>-based age distributions were constructed as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Vanneste, K., Van de Peer, Y. &amp; Maere, S. Inference of genome duplications from age distributions revisited. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 177â190 (2013)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3505">67</a></sup>. In brief, the paranome was constructed by performing an all-against-all protein sequence similarity search using BLASTP with an <i>E</i> value cutoff of 1âÃâ10<sup>â10</sup>, after which gene families were built with the mclblastline pipeline (v10-201) (<a href="http://micans.org/mcl">http://micans.org/mcl</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Enright, A. J., Van Dongen, S. &amp; Ouzounis, C. A. An efficient algorithm for large-scale detection of protein families. Nucleic Acids Res. 30, 1575â1584 (2002)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3522">68</a></sup>. Each gene family was aligned using MUSCLE (v3.8.31)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3526">69</a></sup>, and <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> estimates for all pairwise comparisons within a gene family were obtained through maximum likelihood estimation using the CODEML program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Goldman, N. &amp; Yang, Z. A codon-based model of nucleotide substitution for protein-coding DNA sequences. Mol. Biol. Evol. 11, 725â736 (1994)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3534">70</a></sup> of the PAML package (v4.4c)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3538">62</a></sup>. Gene families were then subdivided into subfamilies for which <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> estimates between members did not exceed a value of 5. To correct for the redundancy of <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> values (a gene family of <i>n</i> members produces <i>n</i>(<i>n</i>â1)/2 pairwise <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> estimates for <i>n</i>â1 retained duplication events), a phylogenetic tree was constructed for each subfamily using PhyML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Guindon, S. et al. New algorithms and methods to estimate maximum-likelihood phylogenies: assessing the performance of PhyML 3.0. Syst. Biol. 59, 307â321 (2010)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3568">61</a></sup> under default settings. For each duplication node in the resulting phylogenetic tree, all <i>m K</i><sub>S</sub> estimates between the two child clades were added to the <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> distribution with a weight of 1/<i>m</i> (where <i>m</i> is the number of <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> estimates for a duplication event), so that the weights of all <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> estimates for a single duplication event summed to one. The resulting age distribution of the <i>A. shenzhenica</i> paranome is shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 4a</a>.</p><p>Absolute dating of the identified WGD event in <i>A. shenzhenica</i> was performed as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Cai, J. et al. The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. Nat. Genet. 47, 65â72 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3608">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Vanneste, K., Baele, G., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Analysis of 41 plant genomes supports a wave of successful genome duplications in association with the Cretaceous-Paleogene boundary. Genome Res. 24, 1334â1347 (2014)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3611">12</a></sup>. In brief, paralogous gene pairs located in duplicated segments (anchors) and duplicated pairs lying under the WGD peak (peak-based duplicates) were collected for phylogenetic dating. Anchors, assumed to correspond to the most recent WGD event, were detected using i-ADHoRe (v3.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Proost, S. et al. i-ADHoRe 3.0âfast and sensitive detection of genomic homology in extremely large data sets. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e11 (2012)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3615">71</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Fostier, J. et al. A greedy, graph-based algorithm for the alignment of multiple homologous gene lists. Bioinformatics 27, 749â756 (2011)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3618">72</a></sup>. Their <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> distribution is shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 4b</a>. The identified anchors confirmed the presence of a WGD peak near a <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> value of 1 (the long tail and additional peaks in the anchor pair distribution are most likely due to small saturation effects<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Vanneste, K., Van de Peer, Y. &amp; Maere, S. Inference of genome duplications from age distributions revisited. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 177â190 (2013)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3634">67</a></sup> and the remnants of older WGD events in the monocot lineage, such as the Ï WGD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Jiao, Y., Li, J., Tang, H. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Integrated syntenic and phylogenomic analyses reveal an ancient genome duplication in monocots. Plant Cell 26, 2792â2802 (2014)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3638">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. 47, 1435â1442 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3641">14</a></sup>). We selected anchor pairs and peak-based duplicates present under the WGD peak and with <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> values between 0.6 and 1.4 (dashed lines in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig8">Extended Data Fig. 4a, b</a>) for absolute dating. For each WGD paralogous pair, an orthogroup was created that included the two paralogues plus several orthologues from other plant species as identified by InParanoid (v4.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Ostlund, G. et al. InParanoid 7: new algorithms and tools for eukaryotic orthology analysis. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, D196âD203 (2010)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3653">73</a></sup> using a broad taxonomic sampling: one representative orthologue from the order Cucurbitales, one from the Rosales, two from the Fabales, one from the Malpighiales, two from the Brassicales, one from the Malvales, one from the Solanales, two from the Poaceae (Poales), one from <i>A. comosus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. 47, 1435â1442 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3659">14</a></sup> (Bromeliaceae, Poales), one from either <i>M. acuminata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="DâHont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213â217 (2012)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3665">74</a></sup> (Zingiberales) or <i>P. dactylifera</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Al-Dous, E. K. et al. De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 521â527 (2011)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3671">75</a></sup> (Arecales), and one orthologue from the Alismatales, either from <i>S. polyrhiza</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Wang, W. et al. The Spirodela polyrhiza genome reveals insights into its neotenous reduction fast growth and aquatic lifestyle. Nat. Commun. 5, 3311 (2014)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3678">76</a></sup> or <i>Zostera marina</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Olsen, J. L. et al. The genome of the seagrass Zostera marina reveals angiosperm adaptation to the sea. Nature 530, 331â335 (2016)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3684">77</a></sup>. In total, 85 orthogroups based on anchors and 230 orthogroups based on peak-based duplicates were collected. The node joining the two <i>A. shenzhenica</i> WGD paralogues was then dated using the BEAST v1.7 package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Drummond, A. J., Suchard, M. A., Xie, D. &amp; Rambaut, A. Bayesian phylogenetics with BEAUti and the BEAST 1.7. Mol. Biol. Evol. 29, 1969â1973 (2012)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3691">78</a></sup> under an uncorrelated relaxed clock model and an LG+G (four rate categories) evolutionary model. A starting tree with branch lengths satisfying all fossil prior constraints was created according to the consensus APGIV phylogeny<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="The Angiosperm Phylogeny Group. An update of the angiosperm phylogeny group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG IV. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 181, 1â20 (2016)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3695">79</a></sup>. Fossil calibrations were implemented using log-normal calibration priors on the following nodes: the node uniting the Malvidae based on the fossil <i>Dressiantha bicarpellata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Gandolfo, M., Nixon, K. &amp; Crepet, W. A new fossil flower from the Turonian of New Jersey: Dressiantha bicarpellata gen. et sp. nov. (Capparales). Am. J. Bot. 85, 964â974 (1998)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3702">80</a></sup> with prior offsetâ=â82.8, meanâ=â3.8528, and s.d.â=â0.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Beilstein, M. A., Nagalingum, N. S., Clements, M. D., Manchester, S. R. &amp; Mathews, S. Dated molecular phylogenies indicate a Miocene origin for Arabidopsis thaliana. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 18724â18728 (2010)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3706">81</a></sup>; the node uniting the Fabidae based on the fossil <i>Paleoclusia chevalieri</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Crepet, W. &amp; Nixon, K. Fossil Clusiaceae from the late Cretaceous (Turonian) of New Jersey and implications regarding the history of bee pollination. Am. J. Bot. 85, 1122â1133 (1998)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3712">82</a></sup> with prior offsetâ=â82.8, meanâ=â3.9314, and s.d.â=â0.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Xi, Z. et al. USA 109, 17519â17524 (2012)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3716">83</a></sup>; the node uniting the <i>A. shenzhenica</i> WGD paralogues with the other non-Alismatalean monocots based on fossil <i>Liliacidites</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="RamÃ­rez, S. R., Gravendeel, B., Singer, R. B., Marshall, C. R. &amp; Pierce, N. E. Dating the origin of the Orchidaceae from a fossil orchid with its pollinator. Nature 448, 1042â1045 (2007)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3726">84</a></sup> with prior offsetâ=â93.0, meanâ=â3.5458, and s.d.â=â0.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Janssen, T. &amp; Bremer, K. The age of major monocot groups inferred from 800+rbcL sequences. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 146, 385â398 (2004)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3730">85</a></sup>; and the root with prior offsetâ=â124, meanâ=â4.0786, and s.d.â=â0.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Smith, S. A., Beaulieu, J. M. &amp; Donoghue, M. J. An uncorrelated relaxed-clock analysis suggests an earlier origin for flowering plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 5897â5902 (2010)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3734">86</a></sup>. The offsets of these calibrations represent hard minimum boundaries, and their means represent locations for their respective peak mass probabilities in accordance with some recent and most taxonomically complete dating studies available for these specific clades<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Clarke, J. T., Warnock, R. C. &amp; Donoghue, P. C. Establishing a time-scale for plant evolution. New Phytol. 192, 266â301 (2011)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3738">87</a></sup>. A run without data was performed to ensure proper placement of the marginal calibration prior distributions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Heled, J. &amp; Drummond, A. J. Calibrated tree priors for relaxed phylogenetics and divergence time estimation. Syst. Biol. 61, 138â149 (2012)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3742">88</a></sup>. The MCMC for each orthogroup was run for 10 million generations with sampling every 1,000 generations, resulting in a sample size of 10,000. The resulting trace files of all orthogroups were evaluated manually using Tracer v1.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Drummond, A. J., Suchard, M. A., Xie, D. &amp; Rambaut, A. Bayesian phylogenetics with BEAUti and the BEAST 1.7. Mol. Biol. Evol. 29, 1969â1973 (2012)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3747">78</a></sup> with a burn-in of 1,000 samples to ensure proper convergence (minimum ESS for all statistics was at least 200). In total, 303 orthogroups were accepted, and all age estimates for the node uniting the WGD paralogous pairs were then grouped into one absolute age distribution (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig9">Extended Data Fig. 5</a>; too few anchor pairs were available to evaluate them separately from the peak-based duplicates), for which KDE and a bootstrapping procedure were used to find the peak consensus WGD age estimate and its 90% confidence interval boundaries, respectively. More detailed methods are available in Vanneste <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Vanneste, K., Baele, G., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Analysis of 41 plant genomes supports a wave of successful genome duplications in association with the Cretaceous-Paleogene boundary. Genome Res. 24, 1334â1347 (2014)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3757">12</a></sup>.</p><p>To compare the relative timing of speciations and WGD event(s) in orchids based on <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> distributions, we first identified 839 anchors from <i>D. catenatum</i> and 355 anchors from <i>P. equestris</i> using i-ADHoRe 3.0 and calculated their <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> as described above. Identification of orthologues between <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and <i>A. officinalis</i>, <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and <i>P. equestris</i>, <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and <i>D. catenatum</i>, and <i>P. equestris</i> and <i>D. catenatum</i> was performed first by reciprocal BLASTP with <i>E</i> value &lt;1âÃâ10<sup>â5</sup> for proteins from the three orchids and asparagus, followed by sorting BLAST hits by bit-scores and <i>E</i> values. Reciprocal best hits in the four comparisons were selected as orthologues. In this way, we identified 9,142 orthologues between <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and <i>A. officinalis</i>, 10,699 orthologues between <i>A.shenzhenica</i> and <i>P. equestris</i>, 11,386 orthologues between <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and <i>D. catenatum</i>, and 13,139 orthologues between <i>P. equestris</i> and <i>D. catenatum</i>. For each pair of orthologues, ClustalW<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Oliver, T., Schmidt, B., Nathan, D., Clemens, R. &amp; Maskell, D. Using reconfigurable hardware to accelerate multiple sequence alignment with ClustalW. Bioinformatics 21, 3431â3432 (2005)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3838">89</a></sup> alignment was carried out to perform sequence alignment using the parameter for amino acids recommended by Hall<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Hall, B. G. Phylogenetic Trees Made Easy (Sinauer, 2004)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3842">90</a></sup>. PAL2NAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Suyama, M., Torrents, D. &amp; Bork, P. PAL2NAL: robust conversion of protein sequence alignments into the corresponding codon alignments. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W609âW612 (2006)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3846">91</a></sup> was then used to back-translate aligned protein sequences into codon sequences and to remove any gaps in the alignment. Estimates of <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> values were obtained from CODEML in PAML using the Goldman-Yang model with codon frequencies estimated by the F3âÃâ4 model.</p><p>We performed pairwise co-linearity analysis within <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and between <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and <i>A. officinalis</i>, <i>A. comosus</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i>, and <i>A. trichopoda</i>. Homologous pairs of <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and the above species were identified by all-against-all BLASTP (<i>E</i> value &lt;1âÃâ10<sup>â5</sup>), followed by the removal of weak matches by applying a <i>c</i>-score of 0.5 (indicating their BLASTP bit-scores were below 50% of the bit-scores of the best matches)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Putnam, N. H. et al. Sea anemone genome reveals ancestral eumetazoan gene repertoire and genomic organization. Science 317, 86â94 (2007)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3887">92</a></sup>. Then, i-ADHoRe 3.0 was used to identify co-linear segments with parameters as described above except using âlevel_2_onlyâ=âFALSEâ, enabling the functionality to detect highly degenerated co-linear segments resulting from more ancient large-scale duplications (this is achieved by recursively building genomic profiles based on relatively recent co-linear segments). All co-linear dot plots were drawn by selecting co-linear segments according to a specified required number of anchor pairs (given in the figure legend of each of the dot plots). For the comparisons between <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and the chromosome-level assembled genomes (<i>A. officinalis</i>, <i>A. comosus</i>, and <i>V. vinifera</i>) we retained co-linear segments with at least ten anchor pairs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig10">Extended Data Fig. 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Figs 5, 7, 8</a>). For the comparisons with fragmented genomes, like <i>A. trichopoda</i>, and the self-comparison of <i>A. shenzhenica</i>, we kept co-linear segments with five anchor pairs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Figs 3, 4</a>). The start and end boundaries of selected co-linear segments were used to define broader regions containing such segments on the chromosomes or scaffolds by further connecting co-linear segments if they overlapped with each other. Then, duplication depths, that is, the number of connected co-linear segments overlapping at each position of a broader region, were illustrated in the margins of the plots by mapping the connected co-linear segments over each other. The number of anchors required in the co-linear segments could affect the duplication depth in such a way that increasing the number of anchors required tends to remove co-linear segments originating from more ancient WGD(s) due to increased gene loss.</p><p>To identify the duplication events that resulted in the 1,488 anchor pairs in <i>A. shenzhenica</i>, the 839 anchor pairs in <i>D. catenatum</i>, and the 355 anchor pairs in <i>P. equestris</i>, we performed phylogenomic analyses employing protein-coding genes from 20 species, including 12 orchids across all five subfamilies of Orchidaceae (the three orchids with genomes (<i>A. shenzhenica</i>, <i>D. catenatum</i> and <i>P. equestris</i>) plus nine orchid transcriptomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 13</a>)), four non-orchid Asparagales (<i>A. officinalis</i> (genome), <i>M. capitulata</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 13</a>), <i>A. deserti</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Gross, S. M. et al. De novo transcriptome assembly of drought tolerant CAM plants, Agave deserti and Agave tequilana. BMC Genomics 14, 563 (2013)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3961">57</a></sup> and <i>A. cepa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Duangjit, J., Bohanec, B., Chan, A. P., Town, C. D. &amp; Havey, M. J. Transcriptome sequencing to produce SNP-based genetic maps of onion. Theor. Appl. Genet. 126, 2093â2101 (2013)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3967">58</a></sup>), three commelinid monocots (<i>Elaeis guineensis</i>, <i>P. dactylifera</i>, and <i>A. comosus</i>), and <i>A. trichopoda</i>. OrthoMCL (v2.0.9)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J., Jr &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d44148e3984">59</a></sup> was used with default parameters to identify gene families based on sequence similarities resulting from an all-against-all BLASTP with <i>E</i> value &lt;1âÃâ10<sup>â5</sup>. Then, 1,101 of the 2,582 anchor pairs with <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> values greater than five were removed. If the remaining anchors fell into different gene families, indicating incorrect assignment of gene families by OrthoMCL, we merged the corresponding gene families. In this way, we obtained 32,217 multi-gene gene families. Next, phylogenetic trees were constructed for the subset of 777 gene families with no more than 300 genes that had at least one pair of anchors and one gene from <i>A. trichopoda</i>. Multiple sequence alignments were produced by MUSCLE (v3.8.31) using default parameters. These were trimmed by trimAl (v1.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Capella-GutiÃ©rrez, S., Silla-MartÃ­nez, J. M. &amp; GabaldÃ³n, T. trimAl: a tool for automated alignment trimming in large-scale phylogenetic analyses. Bioinformatics 25, 1972â1973 (2009)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d44148e4001">93</a></sup> to remove low-quality regions based on a heuristic approach (-automated1) that depends on a distribution of residue similarities inferred from the alignments for each gene family. RAxML (v8.2.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312â1313 (2014)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d44148e4005">94</a></sup> was then used with the GTR+Î model to estimate a maximum likelihood tree starting with 200 rapid bootstraps followed by maximum likelihood optimizations on every fifth bootstrap tree. Gene trees were rooted based on genes from <i>A. trichopoda</i> if these formed a monophyletic group in the tree; otherwise, mid-point rooting was applied. The timing of the duplication event for each anchor pair relative to the lineage divergence events was then inferred using the following approach (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 20</a>): we first mapped internodes from a gene tree to the species phylogeny according to the common ancestor of the genes in the gene tree. Each internode of the gene tree was then defined as either a duplication node, a speciation node, or a âdubiousâ node. A duplication node is a node that shares at least one pair of paralogues, a speciation node is a node that has no paralogues and is consistent with divergence in the species phylogeny, and a âdubiousâ node is a node that has no paralogues and is inconsistent with divergence in the species phylogeny. Then, if a pair of anchors coalesced to a duplication node, we traced back its parental node(s) until we reached a speciation node in the gene tree. In this way, we circumscribed the duplication event as between these two nodes with the duplication node as the lower bound and the speciation node as the upper bound on the species tree. If the two nodes were directly connected by a single branch on the species tree, the duplication was thus considered to have occurred on the branch. To reduce biased estimations, we used the bootstrap value on the branch leading to the common ancestral node of an anchor pair as support for a duplication event. In total, 628 anchor pairs in 493 gene families coalesced as duplication events on the species phylogeny, and duplication events from 318 anchor pairs in 262 gene families (or from 448 anchor pairs in 367 gene families) had bootstrap values greater than or equal to 80% (or 50%).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Evolution and expression analysis of orchid MADS box genes</h3><p>We identified candidates of MADS-box genes by searching the InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Zdobnov, E. M. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterProScan--an integration platform for the signature-recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics 17, 847â848 (2001)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d44148e4023">51</a></sup> result of all the predicted <i>A. shenzhenica</i> proteins. The candidates of MADS-box genes were further determined by SMART<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Letunic, I., Doerks, T. &amp; Bork, P. SMART: recent updates, new developments and status in 2015. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, D257âD260 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d44148e4030">95</a></sup>, which identified MADS-box domains comprised by 60 amino acids. The protein-sequence set of the MADS-box gene candidates was BLAST against the assembled <i>A. shenzhenica</i> transcriptomes with the TBLASTN program. The matched transcript sequences were then assembled with the candidates of MADS-box genes using Sequencher v5.1 (Gene Codes Corp.) and the exon structure of the final MADS-box genes was manually edited (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Data 1</a>). In the end, we aligned all the identified MADS-box genes using the ClustalW program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Oliver, T., Schmidt, B., Nathan, D., Clemens, R. &amp; Maskell, D. Using reconfigurable hardware to accelerate multiple sequence alignment with ClustalW. Bioinformatics 21, 3431â3432 (2005)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d44148e4041">89</a></sup>. An unrooted neighbour-joining phylogenetic tree was constructed in MEGA5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Tamura, K. et al. MEGA5: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis using maximum likelihood, evolutionary distance, and maximum parsimony methods. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2731â2739 (2011)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d44148e4045">96</a></sup> with default parameters.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Transcriptomic analysis of other orchids</h3><p>In addition, 53 more transcriptomes derived from 9 more taxa and 8 tissues (flower bud, anther, pollinium, shoot, stem, leaf, aerial root and root) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 13</a>) were sampled to investigate the roles of the genes that may be important for the evolution of orchid traits. The gene expression levels were indicated by FPKM on the longest assembled transcript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Data availability</h3><p>Genome sequences and whole-genome assembly of <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and whole transcriptomes have been submitted to the National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) database under BioProject <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA310678">PRJNA310678</a>; the remaining transcriptomes used in this study can be found in the previously available BioProjects PRJNA288388, PRJNA304321, and PRJNA348403; the raw data and the updated whole-genome assembly of <i>P. equestris</i> have been submitted to NCBI under BioProject <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA389183">PRJNA389183</a>; and the raw data and the updated whole-genome assembly of <i>D. catenatum</i> have been renewed under the already existing BioProject <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA262478">PRJNA262478</a>. All other data are available from the corresponding authors upon reasonable request.</p><p>This work is licensed under a Creative Commons Attribution 4.0 International (CC BY 4.0) licence. The images or other third party material in this article are included in the articleâs Creative Commons licence, unless indicated otherwise in the credit line; if the material is not included under the Creative Commons licence, users will need to obtain permission from the licence holder to reproduce the material. To view a copy of this licence, visit <a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by/4.0/">http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by/4.0/</a>.</p></div></div></section>

morphology of orchid flowers.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Illustration of an <i>Apostasia</i> flower. <b>b</b>, class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature23897/figures/6" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature23897/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature23897_Fig6_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 2 Phylogenetic tree showing the topology and divergence times for 15 genomes (<i>A. trichopoda</i>, <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i>, <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i>, <i>Sorghum bicolor</i>, <i>A. comosus</i>, <i>Musa acuminata</i>, <i>Phoenix dactylifera</i>, <i>A. officinalis</i>, <i>A. shenzhenica</i>, <i>P. equestris</i> and <i>D. catenatum</i>) and 10 transcriptomes (<i>Apostasia odorata</i>, <i>Cypripedium margaritaceum</i>, <i>Galeola faberi</i>, <i>Habenaria delavayi</i>, <i>Hemipilia forrestii</i>, <i>Lecanorchis nigricans</i>, <i>M. capitulata</i>, <i>Neuwiedia malipoensis</i>, <i>Paphiopedilum malipoense</i>, <i>Vanilla shenzhenica</i>).</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>The unigenes of the transcriptomes of the 10 âtranscriptomeâ species were aligned to the 439 single-copy gene families of the 15 âgenomeâ species. One hundred and thirty-two single-copy gene families for the 25 species could be identified, and were used to construct a phylogenetic tree based on the PhyML software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Guindon, S. et al. New algorithms and methods to estimate maximum-likelihood phylogenies: assessing the performance of PhyML 3.0. Syst. Biol. 59, 307â321 (2010)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d44148e4345">61</a></sup> with the GTR+Î model, while divergence times (indicated by light blue bars at the internodes) were predicted by MCMCTREE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d44148e4349">62</a></sup>. The range of the bars indicates the 95% confidence interval of the divergence times.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig7"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature23897/figures/7" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature23897/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature23897_Fig7_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 3 Venn diagram showing unique and shared gene families among members of Orchidaceae, dicots, and Poaceae, and <i>M. acuminata</i> and <i>P. dactylifera</i>.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Numbers represent the number of gene families. Comparison of the 4 groups revealed 474 gene families unique to Orchidaceae and which exist in all 3 Orchidaceae species. If we consider lineage-specific gene families for each group (that is, gene families present in one or a few but not all species in a group), then there are 4,958 unique gene families for Orchidaceae, 7,503 for Poales, 4,494 for the dicots, and 1,560 for the group of <i>M. acuminata</i> and <i>P. dactylifera</i>.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig8"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature23897/figures/8" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature23897/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature23897_Fig8_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 4 <i>A. shenzhenica K</i><sub>S</sub>-based age distributions.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Distribution of <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> for the whole <i>A. shenzhenica</i> paranome. <b>b</b>, Distribution of <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> for duplicated anchors found in co-linear regions as identified by i-ADHoRe. A WGD event is identified in both distributions with its peak centred on a <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> value of 1. The dashed lines indicate the <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> boundaries used to extract duplicate pairs for absolute phylogenomic dating of the WGD event (see Methods and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature23897#Fig9">Extended Data Fig. 5</a>).</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig9"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature23897/figures/9" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature23897/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature23897_Fig9_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 5 Absolute age of the <i>A. shenzhenica</i> WGD event.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Absolute age distribution obtained by phylogenomic dating of <i>A. shenzhenica</i> paralogues. The solid black line represents the KDE of the dated paralogues, and the vertical dashed black line represents its peak at 74 Ma, which was used as the consensus WGD age estimate. The grey lines represent density estimates from 2,500 bootstrap replicates and the vertical black dotted lines represent the corresponding 90% confidence interval for the WGD age estimate, 72â78 Ma (see Methods). The histogram shows the raw distribution of dated paralogues.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig10"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature23897/figures/10" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature23897/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature23897_Fig10_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 6 Co-linearity and synteny between <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and <i>A. comosus</i>.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Only co-linear segments with at least 20 anchor pairs are shown. The sections on each scaffold with co-linear segments between <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and <i>A. comosus</i> are shown in grey. The red bars below the dot plot illustrate the duplication depths (the number of connected co-linear segments overlapping at each scaffold/chromosomal position; see Methods). Only connected co-linear segments with at least ten anchor pairs were used to calculate the duplication depths. The co-linear regions in green highlight the four co-linear segments in <i>A. shenzhenica</i> that correspond to a specific set of four co-linear segments in <i>A. comosus</i>, which originated from one of the seven ancestral pre-Ï-WGD chromosomes in monocots (known as Anc6)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. 47, 1435â1442 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d44148e4509">14</a></sup>. The phylogenetic tree above the dot plot indicates how Anc6 evolved into (segments of) the current four chromosomes in <i>A. comosus</i> (the pair of paired LG18 and LG04, and LG13 and LG23; see Figure 2 in Ming <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. 47, 1435â1442 (2015)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d44148e4520">14</a></sup>) through two rounds of WGDs. Names of very small <i>A. shenzhenica</i> scaffolds are omitted for clarity. A part of the alignment of the co-linear segments between <i>A. shenzhenica</i> and <i>A. comosus</i> is shown below. The colours of genes in the alignment indicate anchor pairs with genes of the same colour being homologous. The grey links connect anchor pairs between the two closest segments.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig11"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature23897/figures/11" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature23897/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature23897_Fig11_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 7 Phylogenetic and expression analysis of orchid B-AP3 genes.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Ash, <i>A. shenzhenica</i>; Dca, <i>D. catenatum</i>; Hf, <i>H. forrestii</i>; Mc, <i>M. capitulata</i>; Peq, <i>P. equestris</i>; Pm, <i>P. malipoense</i>; Vs, <i>V. shenzhenica</i>. Expressions of B-class genes derived from <i>H. forrestii</i> are not shown, because only a flower sample was collected from <i>H. forrestii</i>. The expression levels (FPKM value) are represented by the colour bar.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig12"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature23897/figures/12" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature23897/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature23897_Fig12_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 8 Phylogenetic tree of MIKC*-type genes.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>The red boxes indicate MADS-box genes from <i>A. shenzhenica.</i> Ash, <i>A. shenzhenica</i>; Dca, <i>D. catenatum</i>; Hf, <i>H. forrestii</i>; Mc, <i>M. capitulata</i>; Peq, <i>P. equestris</i>; Pm, <i>P. malipoense</i>; Vs, <i>V. shenzhenica</i>. MIKC* sequences of the other species were retrieved from GenBank based on Liu <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Liu, Y. et al. Plant Cell 25, 1288â1303 (2013)" href="/articles/nature23897#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d44148e4631">20</a></sup>.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig13"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/nature23897/figures/13" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature23897/MediaObjects/41586_2017_Article_BFnature23897_Fig13_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Figure 9 Expression patterns of MIKC* MADS-box genes.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Ash, <i>A. shenzhenica</i>; Dca, <i>D. catenatum</i>; Mc, <i>M. capitulata</i>; Peq, <i>P. equestris</i>.             <aside><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__container" data-component-show-more="true" id="editorial-summary"><section><h1 class="c-article-editorial-summary__title u-h3">Editorial Summary</h1><h2 class="c-article-editorial-summary__article-title">Orchid origins</h2><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__content c-article-editorial-summary__content--less"><p>Around 10 per cent of flowering plant species are orchids, with a broad diversity in both morphology and lifestyle. <i>Apostasia</i> is one of the earliest-diverging genera of Orchidaceae. To study the evolution and diversity of Orchidaceae, Zhong-Jian Liu, Yves Van de Peer and colleagues sequenced the genome of <i>Apostasia shenzhenica</i>, a self-pollinating species found in southeast China. The authors also report improved genomes for two species of Epidendroideae, <i>Phalaenopsis equestris</i> and <i>Dendrobium catenatum</i>, as well as transcriptome analysis of representatives of subfamilies of Orchidaceae. Their analyses provide insights into orchid origins, genome evolution, adaptation and diversification.</p></div></section></div></aside>
